💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

The \texttt{lifetime} data set contains ages at death for a group of
individuals. Obtain the histogram of this data set and comment on the
data distribution.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

individuals are older rather than younger, which is sensible in
practice.

  \AttributeTok{xlab =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Age at Death\textquotesingle{}}

\includegraphics{ls4b_plotting_files/figure-pdf/unnamed-chunk-4-1.pdf}

\end{tcolorbox}

\section{Mean and median}\label{mean-and-median}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  When describing numerical data, it is common to report
  \textbf{representative} value(s), where \textbf{representative}
  implies that the data tend to be centered or located around these
  particular value.
\item
  When the data distribution is \ul{unimodal}, common measures to
  describe the data are:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Mean
  \item
    Median
  \item
    Mode (more useful for categorical data)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The \textbf{mean} is the average value of the data. Every observation
  has an equal contribution to the calculation of the mean, i.e., center
  of ``mass.''
\end{itemize}

\[
\bar{x} = \dfrac{x_1 + x_2 + \dots + x_n}{n}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\item
  The \textbf{median} is the middle value when the data are arranged in
  ascending order. It is also known as the \(50\)th percentile or the
  second quartile \(Q_2\).
\item
  The median divides the data into two parts: approximately half
  (\(50\%\)) less than the median and approximately the other half
  (\(50\%\)) greater than the median.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=3.98958in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_median.png}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Calculating the mean is relatively straightforward, while calculating
  the median requires sorting the data. However, the mean is easily
  influenced by skewness and outliers compared to the median.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics{index_files/mediabag/4_mean_median1.png}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  When the data distribution is \ul{multimodal}, the mean and median of
  each sub-population should be reported instead.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=4.46875in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_mixture_2_overall.png}
\end{center}

\section{Quartiles and five-number
summary}\label{quartiles-and-five-number-summary}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Quartiles} are 3 specific percentiles that divide a dataset
  into four parts, with each part containing approximately one-fourth,
  or \(25\%\), of the observations.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \(Q_1\) - First quartile or \(25\)th percentile
  \item
    \(Q_2\) - Second quartile or \(50\)th percentile or the
    \textbf{median}
  \item
    \(Q_3\) - Third quartile or \(75\)th percentile
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=5.19792in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_quartiles.png}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Quartiles are robust to skewness and outliers.
\item
  A \textbf{five-number summary} refers to the following \(5\) numbers
  to summarize the data

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Minimum (smallest value)
  \item
    \(Q_1\) - First quartile or \(25\)th percentile
  \item
    \(Q_2\) - Second quartile or \(50\)th percentile or the
    \textbf{median}
  \item
    \(Q_3\) - Third quartile or \(75\)th percentile
  \item
    Maximum (largest value)
  \end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}

\section{Interquartile range (IQR), variance, and standard
deviation}\label{interquartile-range-iqr-variance-and-standard-deviation}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Variability is also known as spread. Variability refers to how much
  the data values are spread out.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Interquartile range (IQR)
  \item
    Variance
  \item
    Standard deviation
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The \textbf{interquartile range (IQR)} is the difference between the
  third and first quartiles.
\item
  The \textbf{variance} is a measure of variability that aims to
  quantify how much the data values are spread \ul{from the mean}. The
  units associated with the variance are squared and the variance must
  be positive.
\end{itemize}

\[
  s^2 = \dfrac{\sum_{i = 1}^n (x_i - \bar{x})^2}{n - 1}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \textbf{standard deviation} is defined to be the positive square
  root of the variance.
\end{itemize}

\[
s = \sqrt{s^2}
\]

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Unlike the variance, the standard deviation is measured in the same
  units as the data, which is easier to interpret. The standard
  deviation must also be positive.
\item
  The standard deviation roughly describes how far away typical
  observations are from the mean.
\end{itemize}

\section{Summary statistics in R}\label{summary-statistics-in-r}

Summary statistics for a data set can be obtained as follows:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{mean()} gives the sample mean
\item
  \texttt{median()} gives the sample median
\item
  \texttt{min()} gives the minimum value
\item
  \texttt{max()} gives the maximum value
\item
  \texttt{range()} gives a vector containing the minimum and maximum
\item
  \texttt{summary()} gives the five-number summary and sample mean
\item
  \texttt{IQR()} gives the interquartile range
\item
  \texttt{var()} gives the sample variance
\item
  \texttt{sd()} gives the sample standard deviation
\end{itemize}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Find summary statistics of the \texttt{bus\_driver}, \texttt{math\_sat},
and \texttt{lifetime} data sets.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

Here, we show summary statistics of the \texttt{bus\_driver} data set
only. Those of the \texttt{math\_sat} and \texttt{lifetime} can be
obtained in the same fashion. Recall the histogram of
\texttt{bus\_driver}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{hist}\NormalTok{(}
\NormalTok{  bus\_driver,}
  \AttributeTok{main =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
  \AttributeTok{col  =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}darkslategray3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,    }
  \AttributeTok{xlab =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Accidents by Bus Drivers\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{ls4b_plotting_files/figure-pdf/unnamed-chunk-5-1.pdf}

Note that since \texttt{bus\_driver} is right-skewed, we would expect
the mean to be greater than the median because the mean is more
sensitive to skewness and is thus dragged towards the skinny tail. In
contrast, \texttt{lifetime} is left-skewed, we would expect the mean to
be smaller than the median (the skinny tail is on the left). Since
\texttt{lifetime} is symmetric, the mean and median are about the same.

\begin{Shaded}

\section{Box plot}\label{box-plot}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Box plot} is a graphical display of the data based on a
  five-number summary.
\item
  From a box plot, we can learn about the data distribution

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{Typical Value:} The median
  \item
    \textbf{Spread:} The range and interquartile range (IQR) of the
    data. The larger the interquartile range (IQR), the higher
    variability in the data
  \item
    \textbf{Outliers:} Potential outliers are marked automatically
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=5.21875in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_boxplot.png}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Box plot and histogram should both be made to complement each other.
\item
  Side-by-side box plots are used to compare the distribution of a
  numerical variable across different categories.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=4.6875in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_four_boxplots.png}
\end{center}

\section{Box plot in R}\label{box-plot-in-r}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Obtain the box plots of the \texttt{bus\_driver}, \texttt{math\_sat},
and \texttt{lifetime} data sets.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

The box plots of the \texttt{bus\_driver}, \texttt{math\_sat}, and
\texttt{lifetime} data sets can be obtained using the \texttt{boxplot()}
function. The generated box plot is vertical by default, so setting
\texttt{horizontal\ =\ TRUE} will generate horizontal box plot instead.
Note that \texttt{pch\ =\ 16} will mark outliers with solid dots.

\begin{Shaded}

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\end{tcolorbox}

\section{Scatter plot and
correlation}\label{scatter-plot-and-correlation}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Scatter plot} provides a view of the relationship between two
  numerical variables. One may be considered as the \textbf{explanatory
  variable} and the other one as the \textbf{response}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=4.0625in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_scatter.png}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Different colors or point symbols can be used on a scatter plot to
  incorporate information from a categorical variable
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=4.6875in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_iris.png}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \textbf{linear relationship} refers to one that can be approximated
  by a straight line on a scatter plot.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics{index_files/mediabag/4_relationship.png}
\end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Correlation} is a measure to quantify the strength of the
  linear relationship between two numerical variables. Correlation does
  not depend on the units of measurement.
\item
  Correlation is strictly between \(-1\) and \(1\).

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    The closer to \(1\), the stronger the \textbf{positive} linear
    relationship is.
  \item
    The closer to \(-1\), the stronger the \textbf{negative} linear
    relationship is.
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=4.0625in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_corr.png}
\end{center}

\section{Scatter plot and correlation in
R}\label{scatter-plot-and-correlation-in-r}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Scatter plot can be generated using the \texttt{plot()} or
  \texttt{pairs()} function.
\item
  Useful graphical parameters include

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{cex} - adjust the size of data points
  \item
    \texttt{pch} - plotting character (point symbol)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Correlation can be computed using the \texttt{cor()} function.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=3.73958in,height=\textheight]{index_files/mediabag/4_pch.png}
\end{center}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

The \texttt{cars} data set gives \texttt{speed} in mph and stopping
distance \texttt{dist} in ft of cars in 1920s. Obtain the correlation
and scatter plot of \texttt{speed} and \texttt{dist}.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

The correlation of \texttt{speed} and \texttt{dist} implies a strong
positive linear relationship, meaning a faster speed will require the
car to take longer to stop. Their scatter plot show that a straight line
provides a good approximation of their relationship.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{cor}\NormalTok{(cars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{speed, cars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{dist)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
[1] 0.8068949
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{plot}\NormalTok{(cars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{speed, cars}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{dist, }\AttributeTok{cex =} \FloatTok{1.5}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{pch =} \DecValTok{18}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{col =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}hotpink3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
     \AttributeTok{xlab =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Speed (mpg)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{ylab =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Stopping Distance (ft)\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{ls4b_plotting_files/figure-pdf/unnamed-chunk-12-1.pdf}

\end{tcolorbox}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

The \texttt{USArrest} data set contains crime statistics for assault,
murder, and rape in each of the 50 US states in 1973. It also contain
the percent of the population living in urban areas. Obtain the
correlation and scatter plot of variables in the data set.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

The correlation matrix of \texttt{USArrest} shows strong positive linear
relationship between \texttt{Murder}, \texttt{Assault}, and
\texttt{Rape} crimes (to a lesser extent for \texttt{Rape}).
\texttt{UrbanPop} seems to have weaker correlation with the crime
measurements.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{cor}\NormalTok{(USArrests)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
             Murder   Assault   UrbanPop      Rape
Murder   1.00000000 0.8018733 0.06957262 0.5635788
Assault  0.80187331 1.0000000 0.25887170 0.6652412
UrbanPop 0.06957262 0.2588717 1.00000000 0.4113412
Rape     0.56357883 0.6652412 0.41134124 1.0000000
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{pairs}\NormalTok{(USArrests, }\AttributeTok{pch =} \DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{col =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}slateblue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{lower.panel =} \ConstantTok{NULL}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{ls4b_plotting_files/figure-pdf/unnamed-chunk-13-1.pdf}

\end{tcolorbox}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Obtain the scatter plots of the \texttt{iris} data set. Use different
colors and symbols to highlight different species of flowers.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

flowers is

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\includegraphics{ls4b_plotting_files/figure-pdf/unnamed-chunk-14-1.pdf}

A fancier way to use custom colors and different symbols is

\begin{Shaded}

\NormalTok{cols }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}steelblue\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}tomato\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}chartreuse3\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{pchs }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}} \FunctionTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{16}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{17}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{)}

\FunctionTok{pairs}\NormalTok{(iris[, }\DecValTok{1}\SpecialCharTok{:}\DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{], }\AttributeTok{pch =}\NormalTok{ pchs[iris}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{Species], }\AttributeTok{col =}\NormalTok{ cols[iris}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{Species])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\end{tcolorbox}

\chapter{4C: Behavioral Survey from the Centers for Disease Control and
Prevention
(CDC)}\label{c-behavioral-survey-from-the-centers-for-disease-control-and-prevention-cdc}

\section{About the Data Set}\label{about-the-data-set}

The data set is slightly modified from
\href{https://www.openintro.org/book/statdata/?data=cdc}{OpenIntro},

\begin{quote}
The Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS) is an annual
telephone survey of 350,000 people in the United States collected by the
Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC). As its name implies,
the BRFSS is designed to identify risk factors in the adult population
and report emerging health trends. For example, respondents are asked
about their diet and weekly physical activity, their HIV/AIDS status,
possible tobacco use, and even their level of healthcare coverage. The
\href{https://www.openintro.org/redirect.php?go=cdc_data_brfss&referrer=data_set_page}{BRFSS
Web site} contains a complete description of the survey, the questions
that were asked and even research results that have been derived from
the data.

This data set is a random sample of 20,000 people from the BRFSS survey
conducted in 2000. While there are over 200 questions or variables in
this dataset, this data set only includes 9 variables.
\end{quote}

The data set has the following variables:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{genhlth} - General health, with categories Excellent, Very
  Good, Good, Fair, and Poor
\item
  \texttt{exerany} - Whether the respondent exercised in the past month
\item
  \texttt{hlthplan} - Whether respondent has some form of health
  coverage
\item
  \texttt{smoke100} - Whether respondent has smoked at least 100
  cigarettes in their entire life
\item
  \texttt{height} - Respondent's height in inches
\item
  \texttt{weight} - Respondent's weight in pounds
\item
  \texttt{wtdesire} - Respondent's desired weight in pounds
\item
  \texttt{age} - Respondent's age in years
\item
  \texttt{gender} - Respondent's gender
\end{itemize}

The hands-on activities below are adapted from the
\href{https://htmlpreview.github.io/?https://github.com/andrewpbray/oiLabs-base-R/blob/master/intro_to_data/intro_to_data.html}{Introduction
to data lab} developed by Dr.~Andrew Bray.

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Describe the data distributions of variables \texttt{height},
\texttt{weight}, and \texttt{age} in the \texttt{cdc} data set.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

Looking at their histograms, the data distribution of \texttt{height} is
symmetric, whereas the data distributions of \texttt{weight} and
\texttt{age} are right-skewed.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Obtain numerical summaries and box plots of \texttt{height},
\texttt{weight}, and \texttt{age} in the \texttt{cdc} data set.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

Note that there are very extreme values of \texttt{weight}. Some people
are very malnourished while some are heavier than the common belief.
This can be the nature of the data or there are errors. It is best to
consult someone with domain expertise if further analyses are needed.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{summary}\NormalTok{(cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{height)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
   Min. 1st Qu.  Median    Mean 3rd Qu.    Max. 
  48.00   64.00   67.00   67.18   70.00   93.00 
\end{verbatim}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Add a new variable \texttt{bmi} to the \texttt{cdc} data set to
represent body mass index (BMI).

\[
\text{BMI} = \dfrac{\text{weight}~(\text{lb})}{\text{height}~(\text{in})^2} \times 703
\]

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{bmi }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ (cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{weight }\SpecialCharTok{/}\NormalTok{ cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{height}\SpecialCharTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{) }\SpecialCharTok{*} \DecValTok{703}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\end{tcolorbox}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

According to standard guidelines, BMI is categorized as underweight
(less than 18.5), healthy (between 18.5 and 24.9), overweight (between
25 and 29.9), and obese (30 or higher). How many people are considered
underweight in the data set? How many people are considered healthy in
the data set?

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

The number of people who are considered underweight is

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{(cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{weight }\SpecialCharTok{\textless{}} \FloatTok{18.5}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
[1] 0
\end{verbatim}

The number of people who are considered healthy is

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{sum}\NormalTok{(cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{weight }\SpecialCharTok{\textgreater{}=} \FloatTok{18.5} \SpecialCharTok{\&}\NormalTok{ cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{weight }\SpecialCharTok{\textless{}=} \FloatTok{24.9}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
[1] 0
\end{verbatim}

\end{tcolorbox}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Obtain the correlation and scatter plot of \texttt{weight} and desired
weight \texttt{wtdesire}.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

The correlation of \texttt{weight} and desired weight \texttt{wtdesire}
is strongly positive, meaning these measures tend to go in the same
direction. However, it is not very clear whether people tend to like
gaining or losing weights.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{cor}\NormalTok{(cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{weight, cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{wtdesire)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
[1] 0.8000521
\end{verbatim}

is given below.

     \AttributeTok{xlab =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Current Weight\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\AttributeTok{ylab =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Weight Desired\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,)}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Add a new variable \texttt{wdiff} to the \texttt{cdc} data set to
represent the difference between desired weight \texttt{wtdesire} and
current weight \texttt{weight}.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

Note that a positive \texttt{wdiff} implies that the person wants to
gain weight and a negative \texttt{wdiff} implies that the person wants
to lose weight.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{wdiff }\OtherTok{\textless{}{-}}\NormalTok{ cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{wtdesire }\SpecialCharTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ cdc}\SpecialCharTok{$}\NormalTok{weight}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\end{tcolorbox}

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Describe the data distribution of \texttt{wdiff} in the \texttt{cdc}
data set.

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

Most \texttt{wdiff} concentrate below 0, meaning people tend to want to
lose weight than to gain weight.

💻 \textbf{Hands-On}

Compare \texttt{wdiff} of male and female individuals. What conclusion
can you make about how people feel about their current weight?

\begin{tcolorbox}[enhanced jigsaw, colbacktitle=quarto-callout-tip-color!10!white, bottomrule=.15mm, opacityback=0, title=\textcolor{quarto-callout-tip-color}{\faLightbulb}\hspace{0.5em}{Answer}, breakable, colframe=quarto-callout-tip-color-frame, opacitybacktitle=0.6, coltitle=black, bottomtitle=1mm, toptitle=1mm, titlerule=0mm, leftrule=.75mm, arc=.35mm, left=2mm, colback=white, rightrule=.15mm, toprule=.15mm]

The side-by-side box plots indicate comparable \texttt{wdiff} between
male and female individuals. Most values are below zero. From this data
set, it seems like people regardless of their gender tend to want to
lose weight.
